0143038583|3.0|I thoroughly enjoyed reading omnivore's dilemma.<br />As an American who has attempted to eat conscientiously for the past 2 decades I naturally find the realities of our food system to be quite fascinating.<br />I won't go into all the details, as many other reviewers have that base covered, but I will remark that much of what Mr Pullman reveals isn't exactly surprising (if you pay any attention whatsoever to \\"food news\\" in the mainstream) but he puts the facts together in a way that is engaging and compelling.<br /><br />My major source of dissatisfaction is that Mr Pollan summarily dismissed vegetarianism as unworkable.  This is in stark contrast with the exhaustive (yet interesting) research that he put into all the other eating paradigms that he covered.<br />Based seemingly solely on his experiences at Polyface farms (where meat eating is the family's \\"bread and butter\\" in that they support themselves largely by selling meat)  the author essentially declares that eating animals is vital to the entire scheme of sustainable farming.  But that is an odd statement when it is only obvious in the context of the particular model used by Polyface.<br /><br />Speaking of Polyface, it was also a bit annoying and distracting that Mr Pollan seemed so enamored by Polyface farms.  It was a bit reminiscent of a new religious convert.  And it was annoying that he regarded his female friend's reaction to the farm family's Christianity as such a surprise or as being uncool.  Hello?  The book is about food, not religion, and not his female friend.  But if Mr Pollan wants to bring religion into it...  Don't be surprised when your non religious or atheist friends don't exhibit excitement upon entering a home with religious decorations and plaques up on the walls and where they will be expected to participate in saying grace at mealtimes.<br />Let them know ahead of time and let them choose if they want to go through that.  After all, how many Christians would be delighted to sit around saying grace to Ganesha without their consent?<br /><br />But really the summary dismissal of vegetarianism with no reasonable supportive evidence or even a thorough argument was a major disappointment to me because I have been predominantly vegetarian most of my life.<br />What exactly about vegetarianism makes it so unworkable?<br />Such a frustrating oversight in an otherwise decent and important work.

He sees multiple women and retires to the bathroom at work daily to \\"relive some pressure\\". I blind bought this film after hearing so many good things about it. It is dark, it is sexual, however it is about the emptiness of a man who may seem he has it together, yet is very lost. The themes of drugs and sexuality are not for the naive reader, but if I had teenagers I think that it would be something I would read with them. Charlie feels like a real person as you read--like he could be your neighbor or that quiet kid at the back of your high school history class. B001R4MEUM|4.0|Recently got reacquainted with this gem in anticipation of seeing The Musical Box' recreation of The Lamb. Trying to explain the psychedelic and rambling story to friends, I went back to the original liner notes and got the gist of the episodic tale of Rael's death and rebirth, or at least this is my take on it;<br /><br />The story is about a young Puerto Rican graffiti punk in Manhattan named Rael, (Gab-rael, REAL as opposed to unreal?) who is sucked into an alternate dimension and goes through rebirth.<br /><br />One early morning  a lamb mysteriously appears in the middle of Time Square. As it lays down a dark cloud descends, covering everything in a woolly dust cloud.<br />Rael emerges from the subway, having spent the night tagging. The dust cloud appears before him like a wall, reflecting images of Broadway's glorious past as junkies and prostitutes are swallowed by it. The dust catches up with Rael like a windshield hitting a fly and covers him like a crust. He loses consciousness.<br /><br />He wakes up in a cocoon, feeling safe, but outside it is a cage made of stalactites and stalagmites. His brother John appears outside the cage, and walks away silently. The cage dissolves and Rael begins to spin. He wakes up in the factory of Lifeless Packaging, where humans are mass produced, numbered and packaged.<br />Rael sees members of his old gang on the production line, along with his brother John. Rael runs away, up the stairs to a staged version of New York. He starts to remember his past as a juvenile delinquent and his awkward early adventures with girls involving a book on erogenous zones.<br /><br />He cuddles a \\"prickly porcupine\\", a metaphor for his heart, as the next instrumental piece is called hairless heart and the notes says he shaves the porcupine. Or he spanks his monkey, which makes sense if you go with my interpretation of the carpet crawlers.<br /><br />Going down a red corridor with lambswool floor he encounters the Carpet Crawlers, a group of slow moving people (clinging like fleas to the golden fleece) wandering aimlessly around a big room, unable to ascend a spiral staircase leading to the Chamber. They mutter \\"We've got to get in to get out\\" These are representatives of sperm looking to reach the egg in order to be born.<br />Rael climbs the staircase and reaches the Chamber of 32 Doors. Only one leads out, but which?<br />There are advisors, politicians, rich men, farmers, his parents even giving advice on which to choose, but each try brings him back.<br /><br />A pale blind woman named Lilith (the first wife of Adam) takes Rael by the hand and leads him through the right door, through tunnels to a vestibule with a stone throne. She leaves him there and a bright light fills the cave. The walls come crumbling down and buries him. Over the rubble, an personification of death appears, the \\"Supernatural Anaesthetist.\\"<br />in the guise of an embalmer. He does a dance and leaves as Rael crawls out from under the rocks.<br /><br />He follows the scent of perfume down a passage until he reaches a pink watered pool of water shrouded in mist. The perfume grows stronger as three snakelike creatures are swimming towards Rael.<br />Each reptilian creature has the diminutive head and breasts of a beautiful woman. They are the Lamia. (in Greek and Roman mythology they were female demons that preyed on children)His horror gives way to infatuation as their soft green eyes show their welcome. Rael enters the pool and gives in to their caresses, which turn to bites. As the Lamia ingest his blood they start convulsing in pain and die. Rael then proceeds to eat their flesh.<br /><br />Leaving by the same door from which he had come in, Rael  comes upon the freakish Colony if Slippermen, groteque figures covered in ugly lumps and slimy stumps . One of the colony approaches him.<br />Rael is a little disillusioned, when the Slipperman reveals that the entire colony have one-by-one been through the same glorious romantictragedy with the same three Lamia, who regenerate themselves every time, and that now Rael shares their physical appearance and shadowy fate. Amongst the contorted faces of the Slippermen, Rael recognises what is left of his brother John.<br /><br />John bitterly explains that the entire life of the Slipperman is devoted to satisfying the never-ending hunger of the senses, which has been inherited from the Lamia. There is only one escape route; a dreaded visit to the notorious Doktor Dyper who will remove the source of the problems, or to put it less politely, castrate. They decide to go together to visit the Doktor.<br /><br />After the procedure, they wear their severed members in yellow tubes hung around their necks. Suddenly a raven swoops down and carries off Rael's tube. Rael gives pursuit, but John refuses to join in the chase.<br />The raven flies down a tunnel and drops the precious package into a ravine, where a river carries it off. Up above, through a skylight comes the familiar smells and sounds of Manhattan.<br /><br />Forgetting his lost member, Rael rushes for the exit, but stops when he hears a cry for help. Below, in the raging rapids he sees his brother John. As the skylight closes Rael jumps into the river to save him.<br /><br />Pulling John out of the waters, he discovers it's not his brother, it's himself that he rescued. The two bodies dissolve to become part of everything.tragedy with the same three Lamia, who regenerate themselves every time, and that now Rael shares their physical appearance and shadowy fate. B001EAWMGA|4.0|I'm not sure why this fine film didn't get a wide theatrical release; it could be that industry execs are still thinking that Jim Caviezal is typecast from his portrayal of Jesus in \\"The Passion Of The Christ\\".<br /><br />Typecast or not, Jim Caviezal is a first-rate actor.  \\"Outlander\\" is a well-written film, but it's the kind of sci-fi adventure story that no actor goes into thinking that he'll get an oscar nod for his knock-your-socks-off great interpretation of his character ... because the characters, as written, really aren't anyone you haven't encountered before in similar films.<br /><br />Paul Newman said, \\"Give an actor a good script, and he can move the earth.\\"  Caviezal is living proof of this; what puts this particular film over the top, so to speak, is that Caviezal take a reasonably good script and adds a tremendous amount of depth to it.  Without any great monologues to work with, he creates the heart and soul of this script through his interactions with the other characters in the story, as a man from the future, learning to inhabit the past.  He is that rare actor who can successfully portray a quality of indomitability, whilst all the while seeming quite human, with no pretensions towards perfection.  Caviezal is fierce without being boorish or arrogant; and he's a man of flesh and blood, seemingly quite three-dimensional, without relying upon a \\"tragic flaw\\" to convince his audience that he's just as human as they are.<br /><br />In this, as well as other films he's done, Jim Caviezal inhabits not just his character, but also has the dramatic ability to allow his character's culture to inhabit him.  Films like \\"The Stoning of Soraya M.\\" and \\"Frequency\\" show clearly Caviezal's commitment to not just create the person, but to also give the audience an excellent sense of that person's era and culture, particularly in the way both of these express themselves in that person's speech, demeanor, and perspective.<br /><br />Well, that gets tricky here, because Caviezal's character is from the future, and he must acclimate himself to a place in the distant past.  He becomes a hybrid of both worlds; there's just something in the way he behaves that portrays the influences of his own future culture, along with this new (to him anyway) environment of the past in which he should be a fish out of water, but instead he soon becomes a leader.<br /><br />I won't bore you with a re-hash of the plot; read the synopsis if you're curious about that.  What I will tell you is that this is a good screenplay rendered into a fine film by the dramatic gifts of its star.<br /><br />If you're either a sci-fi fan, or, just a Jim Caviezal fan, you'll like this.  What you'll also like is this:  As I'm writing this in April of 2012, principle photography is being shot on \\"The Tomb\\", in which Caviezal will star with Stallone and Schwarzeneggar; he'll play a prison warden.  If successful, hopefully the studio execs will be convinced that Caviezal can open films on his own; an actor with this kind of ability shouldn't be typecast or denied his just and due success.

1594203334|3.0|I just finished reading this book, and took away some clever French parenting tips such as saying to my children: \\"It's me who decides,\\" or \\"You don't have the right\\" to hit your brother...and the idea of giving children \\"framed\\" autonomy. Those were the high points. And I wish that schools in America had the same focus on delicious menus, but alas, we don't!<br /><br />What disappointed me was how the author characterized American children as rude (not saying hello when greeting adults), whiny (kicking their parents in public), argumentative (rather than complying with adults' instructions) and being overly-reliant on their parents (not going to sleep-away camp at a young age). While those traits may characterize a particular cohort of American parents engulfed in modern parenting angst, I would argue that there are plenty of parents in America who raise polite, autonomous, respectful children with broad culinary palates who go away to camp at age 5. Perhaps they are not the stereotypical bratty American children one sees on TV, but I know lots who are just like the ones the author describes as uniquely French, my own children included.<br /><br />So, for that reason, and because the author is an experienced fact-based journalist, I'm disappointed that so much of the premise of the book is founded upon faulty data--using as comparison, Americans she's encountered and who she presumes to be representative of the American way of parenting. It seems too anecdotal--her evidence too spotty and suited toward her argument. (The book seems like it could have benefited from a healthy editing, with a dominant focus on what's good about French parenting, rather than so much comparison with America.) In some places she does insert the inevitable caveat (\\"while not all are like this...\\") but too often she attributes Frenchness to what most people would consider regular 'ole good parenting, regardless of nationality.

B004S3X9ZS|5.0|It was my new cousin Pip from England who introduced me to Metronomy. They are the type of band where you can honestly say they create a musical atmosphere you can really fall under the spell of. Sadly,especially in America,a lot of cultural negativity has somewhat kept the progress of pop music stilted for over a decade. Even during very anti dance music eras such as the early 80's (post disco years) and early 90's (alternative rock years) bands in Europe were still innovating in the dance,electronic and funk music other elements of the culture were rejected. With the advent of indie pop,they actually got what you might call a fighting chance to be...sometimes next to incredible. This multi sexual,multi racial band from Devon England are led by singer/songwriter/multi instrumentalist Joseph Mount. From the get go it's clear their sound was based in electronica. But one look into their history Mount has remixed songs for many people from the Gorillaz to Lady Gaga. So it's only appropriate on this,their debut album that something special was bound to happen.<br /><br />  The overall sound of this album is imaginative and dreamy. It's by no means slow and trippy either. The title track,something of an intro is a moody string based orchestral piece before going into \\"We Broke Free\\",a simple pop number with some...less than simple 80's new wave style synthesizer washes and \\"Everything Goes My Way\\". This song,sung by female singer/drummer AnnaPrior reminds me of a band called Papa's Frita's,a much earlier indie band who made some music that might've helped influenced Metronomy from the sound of things. \\"The Look\\" has this organ powered,spare rock and soul sound that is very poppy and somehow unique as well. \\"She Wants\\" adds a bit of a slow ska/funk flavor to it. Very much from the early Talking Heads/Police school but again more stripped down than either. \\"Trouble\\" brings a doo-wop type rhythm into play while \\"The Bay\\" showcases the disco/funk influence that is in the shadow somewhere on a lot of this album. \\"Loving Arm\\" has a sound where bubbling electronics combine to form different rhythmic patterns. A very compelling sound.<br /><br />  After the somewhat more pop/rock oritened \\"Corrine\\" the closers \\"Some Written and \\"Love Underlines\\" use the same idea of electronic sounds blended together to create spare,rhythmic epics that are less instrumentally theatrical than the Gorillaz but less musically dense. Joseph Mount is really onto something with Metronomy on this album. I haven't heard any of their older albums yet. On the other hand,speaking from this alone they have the potential to be musical innovators. I understand that in this day and age most music made primarily for the purposes of art is somewhat lodged in the underground. Where much commercial music is again encouraged to be very non experimental. This is not by any means universal law. But with Mount's connections to the pop world Metronomy are a band of a type that could easily be both musically potent and commercially successful. Anyway for a band I knew nothing about until a few weeks ago,they have my interest. And they also present a jumping off point (via Wikipedia and YouTube) for the musically curious to seek out others either involved or on a similar creative path. Something worth looking into.adow somewhere on a lot of this album. \\"Loving Arm\\" has a sound where bubbling electronics combine to form different rhythmic patterns. Wolf Hoffman's guitar work is his best since the band's mid-80's glory years and Mark Tornillo's commanding vocals are now so distinct that you no longer think of him as just Udo Dirkschneider's replacement. \\"Hung Drawn and Quartered\\" is a wicked opener for a band that has a lot of them while the title track is a grower with a few changes in tempo and mood and an excellent solo from Hoffman. \\"Hellfire\\" is a solid mid-tempo rocker while \\"Flash to Bang Time\\" may be their heaviest track since \\"Fast as a Shark\\". \\"Shadow Soldiers\\" is a good anthem with a march beat and a memorable chorus. At this point, Stalingrad is a solid but not spectacular disc. Then \\"Revolution\\" kicks in and takes the album to another level. Providing an excellent balance between its furious pace and the band's trademark warlike backing vocals, this is the finest song on the album. \\"Against The World\\", \\"Never Forget\\", and \\"Twist of Fate\\" are all strong anthems with memorable choruses while \\"The Quick and the Dead\\" is super heavy and like \\"Flash to Bang Time\\" is more speed metal. \\"The Galley\\" is a good dramatic closer that has a long and quieter outro.  Since this doesn't contain a knockout track or two, I wouldn't put this on the level of their best work (on the last album, the knockouts were \\"Teutonic Terror\\" and the title song). Still, this is a strong release from Accept and I'd highly recommend it.

As just one example, she packs everything in her backpack for the first time right before leaving and finds she can't even lift it due to its weight. Many of her decisions along the way were a direct result of immaturity and emotional pain. 1586487981|5.0|Over the last couple of years I have read books by Sachs, Easterly, Collier, Moyo, Yunus and others on economic theories and the world's poor (I'm currently a political science major). All were great in their own right, but each author of those books certainly had a lens he or she was looking through that colors their insights in regards to poverty and its solutions. The great thing about Poor Economics is that the authors do not have a lens, they are not trying to apply an overarching theory to all the worlds poor or looking for a \\"magic bullet.\\" What they do is teach us that each problem the world's poor face, big and small, deserves its own theory, and that this case-by-case strategy is the only way the international community can really help global poverty. Banerjee and Duflo do a great job of this, and because of it I found Poor Economics to be the most informative book on the subject that I have read so far.

First, as a scientist, with HeLa cells growing in my laboratory, I can confidently say that nobody ever made a cent on these cells. The cost of obtaining the cells covers the suppliers expense of propagating them. There is no profit. In addition, all you ever need is one vial of cells; since HeLa cells grow forever, the expense is a one time deal. The fact that there are so many HeLa cells around is a testament to their aggressive growth characteristics- not huge purchasing. 1585104205|5.0|Folks, you can stop your search for a Latin textbook.  This is one that really, really works.  A true work of genius.  Honestly, I wish that teachers of modern languages could use this guy's method.  Arabic, for example, sorely needs a Hans Orberg-style textbook writer to remove all the accumulation of grammatical trappings and put them where they belong:  at the end.  Master each chapter completely before moving forward.  And I mean master.  Memorize all the vocabulary, and then re-read each chapter 3 times.  You will then be well on your way to sight reading.  You also need to get the supplemental reader Colloquia Personarum, AND Luigi Miraglia's Fabulae Syrae.  Both of these readers complement Familia Romana and reinforce it.  (Miraglia's Fabulae Syrae is a great condensation of Ovid's stories, and his Latin style is beautiful).  If you are diligent and work hard, you will learn Latin to a degree you never thought possible.  By the end of the book, for example, you are already being exposed to Catullus.<br /><br />However, the first time user does need some guidance on how best to use this book.  There are a lot of supplemental materials offered from Focus Publishing, a publisher which distributes the Lingua Latina series in the US.  They are very nice people, by the way.  Some of these books you need, some you do not.  I got the exercise workbooks, but never used them.  They may work for some people, but I found them not necessary.  So, I am going to go against the conventional wisdom here and say that Exercitia Latina I and II are optional, not mandatory.  BUT this statement comes with a BIG caveat.  Instead of doing the exercise books, you need to READ and RE-READ (aloud at least once) each chapter.  Only in this way will the constructions sink into your head.<br /><br />You want to learn Latin to read Latin, and the more reading the better.  Doing the \\"pensum\\" exercises at the end of each chapter is also optional, in my view (However, you should read thoroughly and digest the grammar explanations at the end of each chapter.  These are models of concision and clarity, even though written entirely in Latin).  Do not get too hung up on inflectional endings and grammar.  This is important!  This has been the punji trap of many a Latin student.  Latin is a language, not a cipher.  Yes, you need to know the grammar, of course.  Native speakers of Latin (other than rhetoricians) never got overly obsessive on inflectional endings, and neither should you.  This will only slow you down.  Far more important is to be able to sight read each chapter fluently.  We are dealing with a language like any other, not a parlor-game cryptogram.  Such an approach does injustice to the language and will inhibit your growth.<br /><br />You should also buy the audio CD Rom for this book, which can be installed on your computer and you can hear the author read the texts aloud.  This is critical to developing your command of the language.  To do this, you have to re-read each chapter multiple times.  Tedious? Yes.  But learning a language, any language, is not for wimps.  So deal with it.  This is a serious project, and if you are would rather fool around, then don't get this book.<br /><br />You should also buy the book \\"Lingua Latina:  A College Companion\\".  Once you have read a lesson the first time, then read the explanations here.  Do NOT read it until you have worked through the lesson first.  This book explains (in English) the grammar that you need to know to progress.  You do need some clear explanations of grammar to fully comprehend what is being thrown at you.<br /><br />You should also get the vocabulary booklet that accompanies this book.  You are going to forget words, and you need to be able to look them up quickly.  When you work though a lesson, write down ALL the words you don't know in a portable notebook and refer to it during the day even when you are not studying.  Then they'll sink in.  You will look like a geek, but who cares.  This is the level of commitment you need.<br /><br />I am now well into part II of this series, which is Roma Aeterna.  Unlike the first volume, Familia Romana, it's more of a reader, and very challenging.  Lots of new vocabulary thrown at you.  But that's what you need!  But I never imagined I'd be able to read Virgil and Ovid after only one year of study.  And it can be done, if you are disciplined and use the right methods.  Be prepared to work on Latin every day for at least 30 minutes with this series for at least 2 years.  If you are dedicated, you can experience the true joy of being connected to the whole of European history.  So, get this book and get cracking.<br /><br />So, in sum, let me be clear about this.  If you really want to learn Latin, use this book and the others in the Lingua Latina series.  If you would rather fool around, look elsewhere.

0143120530|4.0|Think Twice about Upgrading that Iphone <br /><br />Born in Washington D.C. in 1982 and brought up by a public relations firm and a think- tank president, it was only natural for Joshua Foer to pursue new ideas as a journalist.  After graduating from Yale, Joshua Foer was published in National Geographic, The New York Times, and other publications.  The story that inspired his first book, Moonwalking with Einstein began accidently while writing a report on the Hall of Fame for weight-lifters. It made him wonder if there was a Hall of Fame for intelligence.  The closest Hall of Fame for intelligence Foer could find was the Memory Championships. A quote by one memory champion, Ben Pridmore, stating that anyone can maximize their memory inspired Joshua to personally experience maximizing his memory and report about it in Moonwalking with Einstein.  In writing his book, Foer is successful in connecting to the reader by not only reporting critical events and technical explanations but by narrating them in a personal relatable fashion.  His composition and style is fairly casual and simple making his book an easy read about a potentially dry topic.  However, at some points, Foer deducts from the topic of memory by expanding too much on his personal life rather than the science and actual techniques that influenced him.  Overall, Moonwalking with Einstein is a 4 star read for its reliability and intriguing topic of maximizing memory, even though more scientific and technical explanations should have been expanded.<br />  Moonwalking with Einstein begins with Joshua Foer's curiosity for the Memory Championship influencing him to ask his boss if he could cover the championship as his next assignment.  After persuading his boss to let him report on the 2005 Memory Championships for Slate, Foer began by interviewing the source of his inspiration, Ben Pridmore.  This led him to meet a more influential memory champion Ed Cooke who explained that with a year's training Foer could be the next memory champion. Intrigued, Foer agreed to take up Cooke's offer.  The introduction to Foer's official training to maximize his memory began with Cooke's explanation of a basic mnemonic memory technique called the \\"Memory Palace.\\"<br />Before starting his training, however, Cooke wanted Foer to learn about the importance of memory in the past.  Through Cooke, Foer learned that memory used to be crucial in ancient times where everything was stored in the mind and not on paper.  Cooke explained that when writing was finally founded, it first was created as a memory aid, but over the years it began to take over memory's place lowering its importance.  Cooke continued explaining that memory shapes people by passing on human values, virtues, character, behavior, and culture.  Foer learns that all new ideas come from new dendrite connections within the web of neurons inside the brain. Cooke defines this act as internalizing new external material.  After understanding the importance of memory, Cooke explained that the next step was for Foer to understand memory through scientific empirical tests, the evolution of the human mind, and by pushing his memory's ability with the new knowledge.<br />Following Cooke's advice, Foer studied the extremes of memory with corresponding empirical data.  He started with the extreme of memory retention in a man called S who had the ability to memorize anything and never forget it due to a disorder of overlapping senses called Synesthesia.  According to S, every word and number had an associated image and feeling which made everything stick in his mind in an organized linear manner.  From S, Foer learned the power of senses in memorizing.  To maximize his memory, Foer knew he had to start creating his own linear sense machine like S's.  To continue learning about extreme memorizers, Foer went to The Human Performance Lab where Ericsson, a psychology professor, tested memories of experts.  According to Ericsson's research, experts had expanded long-term memories associated with their field which novices lacked.  Here, Foer offered himself as a non-experienced participant in the process of memory training.  From Ericsson, Foer became familiar with empirical evidence that grouping and adding meaning to words was key in memorization.<br />After learning about the best memories, Foer met with individuals with amnesia to study memory deficiencies.  Here, Foer learned that memories always corresponded to time in relation to other memories.  Scientific evidence revealed to Foer that the hippocampus and medial temporal lobes of the brain are crucial in memory; the hippocampus transfers short term memories into long term ones stored in the neocortex.  He also learned that conscious declarative memories had to have a known place and time.<br />Finally after meeting with the extremes, Foer learned about the specific evolution of the brain and memory.  Cooke explained that understanding how the mind evolved can guide Foer.  He began with an explanation about ancestral essential memories with links to survival.  According to Cooke's research, our ancestral survival depended on remembering locations and images which is why the technique of the \\"Memory Palace\\" was so effective.  From this moment on, Foer began formally learning and practicing the Memory Palace tricks for the Memory Championship by changing everything from random words to numbers, to elaborate images with specific locations.  He improved his memory by sensing each image completely and shutting out external distractions.  Then, during the final months of training, Foer experienced an improving barrier called the \\"OK Plateau.  Foer successfully overcame this moment by refocusing his goals, staying determined, and trying to beat his old memorization record times.<br />Right before the Championships, Foer met with two savants, individuals who are partially psychologically disabled but extremely gifted in memory, Kim Peek and Daniel Tammet.He found out that the commonality in savants was that they had extreme abilities in their right hemisphere (spatial and image) which supported that memories work best with images associated with locations.  With suspicion that Tammet was just an extreme mnemonist, Foer wondered whether we could all be rainmen with the ability to memorize everything.<br />When the Memory Championships finally came, Foer was ready to compete.  He made it through all the rounds and became the final U.S. Champion after just one year of training.  His progress was retested by Ericsson at the Human Performance Lab where his ability to memorize did improve with training.  Foer ends the book with his realizations from his entire journey.  After the whole experience, Foer realized that memory should be deliberatively nourished and continually maintained, because it is what keeps life memorable.<br />From a reader's view, Moonwalking with Einstein is an easy and interesting read for a broad audience due to Foer's casual style and production of multiple attractions for audiences.  Since Foer writes about his own experience and connections with his parents, job, feeling, and new friends throughout the book, he entertains readers even if they are not interested in the real topic of memory.  For instance, he takes a lot of time to explain small insignificant yet entertaining events such as the specificity of Ed's parents, party planning, and celebrating.  These expanded ordinary events allow for the reader to connect to similar discussions and events that occur in a typical person's life making the story more relative for most readers.  However, for readers who are more interested in the science behind the story, this can be a little frustrating, because it distracts from the main attraction of memory.  This issue is expanded, because there are only a few technically scientific explanations provided by Foer regarding memory.  In the few points in the book that Joshua Foer's actually acknowledgesthe science behind memory, he either shallowly defines activities such as neural activity with associated sections of the brain or streams a lot of information without any definition of terms.  The streaming issue can be seen when he simply reports about the amnesia patient S's condition and then states psychological events without much scientific explanation.  This lack of scientific description concerning memory clashes with how well he describes his personal life throughout the book.  Thus, Foer's imbalance to specifics between personal occurrences and scientific findings divide up his audience favoring less scientific readers.  Another audience made up of the individuals who want to learn the secrets to maximizing memory is also attracted to this book. However, if the tricks are the motive behind reading the book, a reader may be partially peeved if he or she is not patient.  It isn't until one third of the book that Foer actually begins to explain the Memory Palace trick which initially catches readers' attention within the first chapter. However in this case, Foer explains the Memory Palace very well with an activity for the readers to try and learn the technique themselves which is very interesting.  One issue with this is that even though he does a thorough job explaining this trick, his explanation of the number and card tricks are not as thorough and therefore less successful.<br />Overall, even though it is not as scientific and in depth as some would like, Joshua Foer's Moonwalking with Einstein is a very relatable read for many audiences due to its focus on personal events, simple explanations for complex topics and interactive Memory Palace trick,.  Due to the fact that it explains the importance of memory and how understanding the brain can help improve our lives during a time when human memory is replaced by handheld technological devices, Moonwalking with Einstein should be considered a 4 star book for its influential ability.

0143121006|5.0|The mutilated corpse of a foreigner found at the base of Fox Tower on January 8, 1937 posed a special problem for Peking police.  The victim was a free-spirited young woman named Pamela Werner.  When Pamela wasn't attending school in Tientsin, she lived in Peking with her adoptive father, Edward Werner, a scholar and former British consul.  She had been beaten to death and then dumped at Fox Tower.  Multiple wounds were inflicted post-mortem in an apparent attempt to dismember the body.  Sections of her skin and some of her organs had been removed.<br /><br />The task of investigating the crime fell to Han Shih-ching, with the assistance of Detective Chief Inspector Richard Dennis, who headed the police in the British Concession in Tientsin.  Dennis delved into Edward Werner's troubled past, learning of the problems he caused in his various diplomatic postings before he got sacked, a history suggestive of mental instability.  Gossip -- the favorite sport of the expat community -- suggested that death and tragedy were Werner's constant companions, including the suspicious death of his wife.<br /><br />A little more than a third of the narrative has passed by before a promising suspect emerges, but if solving the crime were that easy there would be no story to tell -- at least not a story filled with drama and intrigue.  Fortunately for the reader (less so for Han and Dennis), the British government increased its efforts to impede Dennis' investigation, suggesting that a cover-up, if not a full-blown conspiracy, was afoot.  Brits Behaving Badly becomes a subtext, as does the concept of \\"saving face,\\" a characteristic often associated with Asians but quite applicable to the British living and working in China.  Racial bigotry also played a role in the British government's insistence that the investigation should focus on Chinese rather than foreign residents.<br /><br />The investigation took place as Peking prepared for invasion by the Japanese.  As in any complex investigation, Dennis and Han pursued a number of false leads.  The investigation brought them into contact with foreign residents of Peking who indulged in (to put it delicately) unusual recreational activities, suspicious but not necessarily related to Pamela's murder.<br /><br />A little more than halfway through the narrative, Dennis finally receives information that provides a credible solution to the mystery while pointing to a suspect who is beyond the law's reach.  At that point, however, Peking is virtually under siege by the Japanese and Pamela's disappearance is all but forgotten.  Dennis is recalled to Tientsin, the official investigation is closed, and it falls to Werner to use his own resources to discover the truth about his daughter's death.  He pursues that goal relentlessly over the course of several years.<br /><br />Midnight in Peking reads like a well-paced murder mystery, but it is ultimately a tale of corruption, not just within the Peking police but, more startlingly, within the British government, whose officials valued the faade of British civility more than the truth.  The narrative proceeds at a steady pace and is enlivened by insightful examinations of the principle players.  Paul French provides the reader with enough background facts to add flavor but not so many as to bog down the narrative in needless detail.  The text is well-documented in a series of endnotes.  It seems likely that, for the sake of good story telling, French re-creates some scenes and conversations in greater detail than the historical record allows, but the book suggests no reason to believe that he has plays fast and loose with historical fact.  His attempt to tie the \\"fox spirit\\" into the story -- representing a woman who beguiles and betrays -- is colorful but a bit weak.  Still, Midnight in Peking is a fascinating look at a forgotten moment in a distant land, an unsolved murder that \\"slipped from history\\" despite the compelling evidence of guilt that Werner finally assembled, and that French faithfully reproduces.ally assembled, and that French faithfully reproduces.

0345803485|5.0|Why all the negative reviews for such a compelling book?  Sure, it's not a literary triumph: the writing and the characters have flaws, the characters are somewhat unrealistic, and the dialogue is totally repetitive to the point of annoyance, BUT...this book is every woman's fantasy (with icing on it)!  I don't really see this as a BDSM book, but more of a heavy romance novel with some erotic and interesting sex scenes built in.  The romance is really what drives the novel...the all-consuming \\"I can't live without you\\" type stuff that most woman never really get to experience in life.<br /><br />This is how I judge how good a book is:  how many days did it take me to read it (two days!  And that's saying something for a how busy I am!)?  How much of a page-turner is it (very much so!)?  Do I care about what happens to the characters and how it ends (YES!)?<br /><br />With all its flaws, this book kicks major butt and deserves all the hype it has been getting.  I sort of classify it as 'fantasy romance' - which is just fine with me.  Books are supposed to take you away into a different world - but still allow you to relate and picture yourself in such an unlikely scenario.  And, would I love to be Ana...lemme tell you!  Does it matter that it's not technically the best constructed novel?  Not to me...but it looks like it does matter to a lot of people!  It's just not the way I value books, I guess.<br /><br />In my head, I compared the character of Ana to the character I read in the Marriage Plot.  Both are pretty, smart yet bland gals graduating college, like their English literature, and have boys pining after them that they don't give the time of day.  The Marriage Plot had an interesting concept, was well-written (technically), but it was an effin' bore.  I didn't care about the lead characters, nor did I give a hoot how it ended. I had to ease my way into this workout for that reason, and usually did 3 days in a row with a day off in-between, combining the workout with some walking on the treadmill until my knees got stronger.  Now, after completing the series, my knees seem to be stronger than before because the muscles in my legs that support them are much stronger and more stable.  Because of the high impact moves, this is a tough workout for some of us older folks, but if you exercise patience, I think it is a great stability builder.  Level 1 kicked my butt for the first few days, and I am already used to doing cardio 4-5 days a week.  Level 2 got me for a couple of days but I adjusted to it easier than level 1.  Level 3 forced me to yell at the DVD and on the 10th day I was glad it was over for awhile.  I found level 3 much more difficult, as it started incorporating some jump training.  I have moved on to No More Trouble Zones now, but plan to go back through the Shred again at some point, alternating between levels each time instead of doing 10 days straight of each level.<br /><br />I was not overweight when I began, just a little jiggly in those places we don't like.  I do not follow Jillian's diet plan, but I do follow my own low carb / lower calorie plan. I consider myself to be somewhat metabolically challenged, so I combined my Shred workout with 30-40 minutes of walking or light treadmill jogging.  I have not weighed or measured because I want to focus on being healthy and strong, but my well-developed muffin top has diminished significantly, and I have experienced noticeable changes in my arms, back, abs, glutes, and ankles (last year's sandal straps are too big). B004UXUUJE|4.0|This overlooked gem of a film begins with American journalist Julia Jarmond (the always impeccable Kristin Scott Thomas) searching for information about French Jews captured during WW II.  She is married to a career focused Frenchman.  They are about to move into a new apartment in Paris.  She learns that there is a history with the apartment which was given to the couple by Julia's father-in-law.<br /><br />Through internet information, visits to encampment sites and personal interviews she begins to uncover secrets of what was known as the Vel d'Hiv round-up.  This was not a Nazi orchestrated maneuver but one instituted by the French.  Many Frenchmen believed that imprisoning Jews would save their own hides.  The French would like to ignore this terrible event.  Julia begins to focus on one 10 year old girl named Sarah.  In a haunting performance by Melusine Mayance, Sarah is the lone survivor of her family that was rousted from the apartment 60 years earlier. The apartment that Julia will soon occupy.<br /><br />The film flashes back and forth between the current day investigation and 1942 when the horrible incident occurred.  Sarah carries with her a tremendous burden involving her 4 year old brother as the film moves into the 1960's.  The final act does moves away from the mystery and horror as Julia locates descendents of Sarah and her family.  A bit melodramatic toward the end?  Sure, but it felt somewhat earned as release from the pit-in-stomach feeling that had been built up.  A very good movie.<br /><br />Scott Thomas not only performs the film with her accomplished French but a damn good American accent as well.  The movie is in French and English with subtitles.  The Blu ray disc has a beautiful 1080p transfer and captures the original aspect ratio of 2.35:1.  There is very little guessing as to which time period you're seeing as the look is much different.  The more somber greyish and browns play during the 1942 period.  The current period involves more color and brightness.  The Blu ray is excellent in highlighting the differences.  The lossless DTS-HD Master Audio is superb as well.  Clear in both languages.  Well executed disc.color and brightness.  The Blu ray is excellent in highlighting the differences. B002RKSZWG|4.0|It feels a bit silly to write a review of one of Jane Austen's last published and perhaps lesser known novels.  Anyone looking at Persuasion has certainly read Pride and Prejudice or Sense and Sensibility, and has a good idea what to expect.  But I'd like to get a few impressions down.<br /><br />Persuasion focuses on Anne Elliot, middle daughter of Sir Walter Elliot and the only person in her family who isn't completely proud and self absorbed.  In her youth, Anne was persuaded to break off a relationship with a naval officer that her family felt was beneath her station.  Eight years later her former beau, Captain Wentworth, is thrown back into her life, much to her discomfort.  She is forced to navigate a passel of family issues and potential suitors in order to find a happy match.<br /><br />There are all the usual elements you can expect in an Austen novel.  It is a wry, incisive look at the foibles of human nature; in this case, a contrast between those who value a person purely on their birth or station, versus those who look to personality and character.  It has various changes of scenery, from the Elliots' traditional home of Kellynch, to the seaside town of Lyme, to Bath, and draws in a host of well drawn characters.  The plot is relationship centered, with one good crisis to drive things forward, and, as I've come to expect, much of it hinges on misunderstanding or deception by characters that aren't all they appear to be on the surface.<br /><br />I liked the book, but I can't say I liked it as much as Austen's more popular novels.  Elsewhere she seems to have written even her foolish characters with a sympathetic hand; we laugh at them, perhaps we pity them, but nowhere else to they come off as so one-sidedly unpleasant as in Anne's father and sisters.  And, by contrast, Anne is almost the perfect heroine from the start, with little room to grow and few mistakes to learn from.  I'm happy I finally read Persuasion--it was the one Austen novel I hadn't read--but it's not the first one I'd recommend.

You think you've seen it all before? Well, brace yourself - you're in for another treat!<br /><br />There's literally nothing Sophie Kinsella cannot do. I picked up I've Got Your Number the other night, thinking I'd read a few pages before going to bed. Three hours and 130 pages later I was still clutching my Kindle, not wanting to let go. I swear I say this every time I finish one of her books but this has to be my favourite novel of hers so far. Well, maybe it's a tie between this one and Twenties Girl, I don't know. All I know is, it's unputdownable.<br /><br />I'm trying not to sound like an obsessed fangirl here (and failing miserably) but it really is that good. There's a bit of a similarity between her books in terms of characters and plot but do you know what? I don't even care. Poppy is an entertaining protagonist who, along with Sam, makes this book as good as it is. If you've read Kinsella's novels before then you know what to expect: a laugh-out-loud plot, witty and funny characters, laughter, and some tears along the way. There's only a few authors who manage to make me smile and cry at the same time and Kinsella is one of them.<br /><br />Character-wise, I would say Poppy and Sam were my two favourites. Poppy is a bit clumsy, someone who tends to feel inferior to others and rarely dares to stand up for herself and confront other people, even if she's right. I loved how much she changed by the end of the novel. Sam is your typical businessman who has confidence, power, but at the same time, some of his comments just made me laugh out loud - and as it turns out, there's a more vulnerable side to him as well.<br /><br />As for the plot, all I'm going to say is that it's brilliant. It's full of twists and turns and I almost never say this but I wouldn't mind if there was a sequel to this book. You know that feeling when you find a really good book and by the time you finish it, it's as if you had to say goodbye to your best friends? That's how I feel about Poppy and Sam, and Kinsella's characters in general. I just didn't want the story to end.<br /><br />I would strongly recommend this book to anyone who's looking for an entertaining read or a swoon-worthy romance - I guarantee you won't be disappointed. It's another masterpiece from the `Queen of Chick Lit' - simply perfect. I just can't praise this enough. Do pick it up and you'll see I'm right.ost never say this but I wouldn't mind if there was a sequel to this book. B0079XNWQA|4.0|In reading other reviews of \\"Love is a Four Letter Word,\\" I have noticed two main threads: those who criticize Mraz for more fully embracing the peace-and-love vibes that made him popular with the release of \\"I'm Yours\\" ca. 2008, and those who admire his willingness to take a new direction and still be playful and creative with the music. In either case, I think we can all agree that \\"Love\\" is a far cry from Mraz's days a curbside prophet, a geek in the pink, or a dynamo of volition. Then again, in reminiscing on his old albums, I see that a lot of those tracks in isolation could make Mraz seem just as flat and over-produced as many accuse him of being now. The truth about Mr. A-Z is that you really do have to be a dedicated fan to understand what he is capable of and to overlook some of the indiscretions that happen in the studio. Because I believe Jason to be a true musician, he is most definitely better in a live setting, where the creativity and spontaneity that make him so lovable are given freer expression. There is no doubt that he is talented, but I will admit that I had my doubts about whether or not I would like this album. In the words of a friend who dislikes \\"I'm Yours\\" just as much as I do, the world does not need another Jack Johnson. While I love the idea of a man and his acoustic guitar, it is easy for that kind of music to slide into the realm of the utterly uninspired.<br /><br />All that having been said, I do not regret purchasing this album. Per the usual, Mraz is ultimately successful in delivering songs that are lovely and worth the listen, even if some are less memorable. I agree with other reviewers that songs like \\"Living in the Moment\\" remind us that Jason is still trying to meet the expectations of younger fans who were converted to his style in the wake of \\"I'm Yours\\" and its startling popularity. I know there are a lot of \\"I Won't Give Up\\" fans out there as well, but I write that off as the perfunctory popular hit single that I normally don't appreciate as much anyway.<br /><br />If there is one reason I can recommend this album to you, it's as simple as this: it speaks to me. While it's true that the lyrics of \\"Love\\" are far less playful and quick-witted than we've seen on previous albums, they are still very rich in their simplicity and, in typical Mraz fashion, self-aware. While a lot of the topics he takes up (love, peace, freedom, family) could be deemed worn-out or cliche, he finds a way to deliver the message in a way that is refreshingly sincere. Really. I believe him when he says something as simple as \\"It's not hard for me to love you,\\" and I realize how comfortably that line made its way into my heart. I really have internalized several of the songs on the album, as much for the message as for the music. For example, I probably listened to \\"93 Million Miles\\" at least two dozen times on the very evening that I bought the album; it was a powerful reminder that even when our sources of light seem so far away, home is always close at hand. The whole tenor of the album has to do with the art of living abundantly, which we come to learn is almost indistinguishable from the gift of loving abundantly. There is nothing trite about that, at least not in regards to Mraz's delivery.<br /><br />Other favorites of mine: \\"The World As I See It,\\" which has what a YouTube commenter refers to as a \\"kind of 70's earnestness to it.\\" I love the vibe and the texture. Similarly, \\"Be Honest\\" (feat. Inara George) was a treat for me; I would take this over the Colbie Caillat duet any day. It almost has a bossa nova feel to it, which makes it perfect for shooting the breeze out on the porch. \\"5/6\\" is another good track for those interested in Jason's jazzy side, and it is one of the more musically complex offerings on the album. I love the horns on \\"The Freedom Song\\" (they were my first smile of the album, maybe because they reminded me of \\"Make it Mine\\").<br /><br />All in all, a good album to remind us that \\"we've got some joy in this thing\\" and that \\"the world... is a remarkable place.\\" For anyone still on the fence about it, I would implore you to \\"Hear just what your heart has to say\\" because this album really will speak to your heart if you let it. It may or may not meet your standards of sophistication if you're a music snob, but it is honest, and that means worlds to me as a music-lover, a twentysomething, and someone who believes in feeding the soul. Jason will do right by you.

Entertaining and informative nonetheless.<br /><br />* Packaging/menu - typical BD case, non-digipak. Menu is easy enough to navigate but a little generic for 2012 - what's with the X's in the corners? I guess you could say this makes it PC for a PC ... ?<br /><br />Well, there you have it. To sum up, excellent PQ, nice set list and performance from Joe and the band (Mike Keneally on keys makes an awesome contribution to the sound), disappointing 5.1 and 7.1 SQ in 2D, decent extras, all in a single disc package. Four stars rather than five due to the surround sound issues I experienced.

0380730405|5.0|The tagline of this book, \\"the classic tale of romantic suspense\\", is rather misleading. False advertisement, if you will. It's not a romance novel. Perhaps, some readers see it that way, but I most certainly do not. Yes, there is a love interest, but there isn't much romance. It's really a mysterious and intense ghost story without a ghost, as told through the eyes of a psychologically flawed young woman.<br /><br />I am told that the opening line of this novel is quite famous. \\"Last night I dreamt I went to Manderley again.\\" Maybe that line ropes in some readers, but not me. I found the first couple of chapters confusing and disorienting. Perhaps that is the author's intention -- to disorient the reader before placing her in a surreal time and place. I don't know. But, after five chapters, I found my footing and really got into the book.<br /><br />At the beginning of the story, the narrator accompanies an older American woman who is traveling in Monte Carlo. There, the narrator meets a gentleman, who eventually asks her to marry him. She accepts and then moves into Manderley, the home haunted by the ghost of the gentleman's late wife. Except that there isn't really a ghost, and that makes the book even creepier.<br /><br />Du Maurier masterfully creates an intensely eerie atmosphere full of mystery that verges on horror. The driveway to Manderley isn't just a driveway, it's a serpent. The grounds of Manderley don't just have some flowers, they are rhododendrons of a slaughterous red. Mrs. Danvers isn't introduced simply as the head of the household staff. She is \\"someone tall and gaunt, dressed in deep black, whose prominent cheek-bones and great, hollow eyes gave her a skull's face, parchment-white, set on a skeleton's frame.\\"<br /><br />The words simmer slowly in your mind and insidiously place you in the mind of the highly anxious and frightened narrator. The characterization is spot on. Our young female narrator begins as a typical youth, anxious to please, uncertain, and self-conscious - traits that are somewhat relatable. But then, du Maurier inches the characterization outside the realm of normal so slowly that you almost don't notice it until you've been inside the head of a psychologically disturbed woman for the past hundred pages.<br /><br />I highly recommend this well-written novel. The mystery and tension are layered so thickly that reading about a ringing telephone actually made me jump just as much as in a horror movie. Crafting that level of intensity is no easy feat, and du Maurier has done so masterfully.ncertain, and self-conscious - traits that are somewhat relatable. But then, du Maurier inches the characterization outside the realm of normal so slowly that you almost don't notice it until you've been inside the head of a psychologically disturbed woman for the past hundred pages.<br /><br />I highly recommend this well-written novel. The mystery and tension are layered so thickly that reading about a ringing telephone actually made me jump just as much as in a horror movie. Crafting that level of intensity is no easy feat, and du Maurier has done so masterfully.

Line for line and paragraph by paragraph, skipping nothing and taking me days and days longer....& I found not only much to appreciate but much to celebrate that I had either skimmed thru, overlooked or even ignored the first time through. Jewels like these: (MANY SPOILERS!)<br />  1) Janos Slynt's deserved beheading--I think I actually cheered out loud!<br />  2) Ser Selmy's hand-to-hand combat in Hizdahr's chambers with Hizdahr's guard...who knew the old dude had it in him? Again, found myself huzzah-ing out loud!<br />  3) Tormund's easy banter with Jon Snow & his shining native intelligence, virtually unmatched by most of the pretentious \\"lords\\" south of the Wall<br />  4) Cersei's \\"Jane Shore\\"-type Walk of Shame, especially when paralleled with her grandfather's mistress's \\"Madame Du Barry\\"-type Stripping of Worldly Goods Bestowed & Proper Booting off the Lannister Property, once the old man died---2 events straight from the history books & borrowed to great effect here<br />  5) Moquorro's fortuitous rescue from the mast of the sinking ship (a great visual there), and his healing by fire of Victarion's arm, potentially foreshadowing yet another way Jon could be \\"healed\\", as opposed to dying/warging/wighting/pulling a Dondarion?<br />  6) The \\"Ugly Little Girl's\\" poisoned coin assasination...in addition to the novel, excruciatingly descriptive way a selected face \\"adheres\\" to one's own.  Enthralling!<br />  7) The Freys/Manderlys/Boltons getting cabin fever in the Great Hall at Winterfell & constantly at one another's throats, especially when the off-stage murders start in thick & nasty<br />  8) \\"Pork Pie\\" served Ser Manderly-style....might have put me off pot pies forever!<br />  9) Missandei hearing, when no one else does, the dragon Viserion scratching himself a cave out of the pyramid wall<br />  10) Bran \\"possibly\\" calling to Theon in the Godswood?<br />  11) Val walking out of the north wood, her task for Jon complete, Ghost by her side, regal & glorious as hell!<br />  12) Pycelle finally biting the dust, after 5 long books wherein he was a constant cringing worm---even excusing his age<br />  13) The vivid battle in the Deepmotte woods between the Ironborn & Stannis's hill tribes...for a few minutes, I wasn't sure whose side I was actually on, & I'm still sure it was Asha's!<br />  14) Davos Seaworth off in search of Rickon, on his way to a very bad place<br /><br />  And much, much more!  I almost forgot Dany's annoying regressive characterization (THIS was the woman who fired life into the dragons?), Tyrion's mostly pointless adventures which went nowhere (the female dwarf is eye-rolling in the extreme!), Jon's constant wrongness when he is trying to make \\"noble\\" decisions (& his tasteless \\"demise\\"---how can someone rewrite the \\"Jane Shore\\" history so elegantly and yet fail so miserably at the \\"Ides of March\\" retelling?), the hugely-missed near-absent Jaime, the abysmal drama & death of Quentyn Martell, & most of all, I nearly forgot the too-convenient plot device of Aegon the Actually-Alive-&-Breathing!<br />  I give this book 4 stars for being HUGELY entertaining & original regarding almost all the side characters, butmust skip a 5th star for the lack of forward progression (& unnecessary regression) of the main characters. I am NOT one of you who waited so ungodsly long for this book. however, so I understand where you may be particularly disappointed in plot movement...particularly after Book 4 also went nowhere fast.  That may be worth a star's difference right there.<br />  Still, GRRM can write, decidedly well, & for the above shining moments & others, especially when thinking of the fantasy genre as a whole, I come away favorably inclined---after my 2nd reading!

We are getting the unedited or embellished sequence of events from the source. So, yes it is going to be a red hot mess. 0062183591|5.0|Cheating is stealing.<br /><br />It's a polite social fiction for when we do something that doesn't seem that bad or dishonest.<br /><br />It's so widespread and accepted that we use it even when there's nothing bad involved - (\\"That store is always so busy during the day that I cheated and went first thing in the morning\\").<br /><br />And the way we justify it is to say that we're not cheating individuals, but more the system, or some \\"thing\\" like the IRS where cheating is widespread. Of course, in that case, you're not cheating other taxpayers, you're just cheating the IRS.<br /><br />And one of the best people to write a book about it (cheating and the IRS), is Dan Ariely. I've really enjoyed his other two books on irrational thinking, and this one doesn't disappoint. He has a knack of showing us how we ignore any signs that might make us feel uncomfortable, and we can rationalize our thinking processes, even if they are irrational. But cheating is a way of doing something clearly wrong, as opposed to sloppy decision-making.<br /><br />Dan has his usual range of test sessions. He analyzes people working under closely controlled circumstance, where cheating is next to impossible. This gives him a baseline, and it's reasonable to assume that if a similar bunch of people are allowed to work unsupervised, or can report their own scores, any increase must be due to cheating. And cheating shows up in just about every situation where it's possible. He changes his experiments to mirror real-world situations, such as having an \\"outsider\\" appear to cheat, or having a set-up similar to people working in groups at businesses.<br /><br />The inspiration for the book was a consultant for Enron (and a Grateful Dead lyricist - John Perry Barlow) who, right up until the collapse of the company, thought nothing was wrong. But when Enron went under he realized that the signs were easy to see and had been around for a long time. As Dan points out, \\"We lie to everyone - especially ourselves.\\" I've been in businesses where the company was in a precarious position, but we all fooled ourselves into pretending all was well - because we wanted to.<br /><br />Dan's sense of humor is well-evident. After admitting in his last book that there was a guy in the experiment who would come on to female subjects to affect the results of the tests, he admitted that he was that guy, and he did it \\"for the good of the experiments.\\" This time around he discovers that creative people are more likely to cheat. And he ends that chapter by saying \\"By the way, I'm not sure if I mentioned it, but I think that I am incredibly creative and highly honest.\\"<br /><br />But the high spot of the humor in this book (and there is lots of it) is when he discovers that people are noticeably more honest when they sign a notice at the top of a sheet, saying they affirm that the information they give is honest. He decided to give that information to the IRS, and there's hilarious interchange where the IRS seems disinterested in changing a form that might have netted them millions, if not billions.<br /><br />There's much much more in this book. It never drags and amazes you with the depths he plunges to get experiments that reflect everyday life. In fact, the only kind of cheating he doesn't investigate is having affairs.<br /><br />So, a great book whether you've read the other two or are just starting reading Dan's books. And he gives good credits to his research assistants, unlike many writers who just mention their name in the Acknowledgements.rselves.\\" I've been in businesses where the company was in a precarious position, but we all fooled ourselves into pretending all was well - because we wanted to.<br /><br />Dan's sense of humor is well-evident. Panem is divided up into the Capitol and 13 Districts.  There was an uprising in the Districts about 74 years earlier that was put down by the Capitol.  In the process of this, District 13 was completely annihilated and the rest of the Districts left to eke out their lives in service to the Capitol, whilst annually supplying a lottery-chosen boy and girl between 12 and 18 years of age to take part in the Hunger Games. These Games are required viewing by all and are designed to remind the greater population that they are subject to the Capitol, with the victor being the last \\"tribute\\" left alive.  It's a fight to the death.  The main character Katniss finds herself thrust into the Games after she volunteers to take the place of her 12 year old sister Primrose as the District 12 tribute.  And so the Games begin.    The Hunger Games presents such a bleak picture of life in Panem.  I found myself hoping from the start that they would rise up and overthrow the Capitol. The story is told in first person present tense, which took me a little getting used to, having never read a book in this tense before.  I think it adds to the pacing of the book though. Fifty Shades of Grey started out as Twilight fan fiction--very naughty Twilight fan fiction.  As the author developed and revised, the characters became much more than Edward & Bella and the story became something completely different.  The result is that even though there are no vampires and a heck of a lot more \\"adult\\" behavior, the adult fans of Twilight are getting their knickers in a twist over these books.  :)<br /><br />Okay, history lesson over. The truth is, when I heard that an indie published, BDSM erotic romance series scored a $5 million movie deal--I had to see what the fuss was about.<br /><br />You can see the echo of Edward & Bella in Christian & Ana, but C&A were much more developed and (for the most part) realistic.  Christian was the more interesting of the two by far.  His psychological issues were fascinating.  He could be ruthless and controlling one minute and completely vulnerable the next, but it was done is such a realistic and believable way.  Watching him grow and evolve throughout the three books was a joy.<br /><br />Ana was stronger and more likable than Bella, but I had a few issues with her character.  First, she starts off as a 21 year old virgin who is completely innocent and within a very short time, she is begging to be trussed up and spanked.  It was too radical of a shift, too quickly.  Secondly, Ana kept talking about her \\"inner goddess\\" and her \\"subconscious self\\" like separate personalities inside her.  Not only was in incredibly annoying, but each time the author mentioned these, it pulled out of the story.<br /><br />Another thing that was a little off was the dialog. Seemingly evil or mean characters turn out to be less heartless than they initially seemed. Such character development shows the viewer that there are different perspectives on each issue.<br /><br />Spirited Away also deals with important themes/lessons, especially for children. I won't get into the specifics, but time and time again, the characters learn that love and friendship are far more valuable than material goods. B005HP2JAM|5.0|I live in North Carolina and I took a trip to NYC to see this film. Why? Aside from the obvious (3D is better in theaters, and it wasn't playing in my hometown, and I'm a dork), I went because:<br /><br />1) Listening to Herzog's interviews about the film got me excited. It's clear that he intensively researched this subject. For him this wasn't just a neat little history lesson about some quaint cave paintings; he was fascinated and in awe at the beauty of these paintings, and like any good art appreciation, he wanted to understand the context around which they were created. He wanted to understand and empathize with the painters' values, fears, and struggles. He went out of his way (to the point of obsession?) to understand these things.<br /><br />2) This was Herzog's most ambitious project since Fitzcarraldo (and there may be no more ambitious project than that one). It was an incredible technical challenge to do this film. Because the cave is a hazard to humans (toxic gasses) and because humans are a hazard to the cave (toxic gasses), Herzog could take only a very small crew into the cave and for only a very brief time. To do 3D filming under these circumstances was a remarkable challenge requiring militaristic-like planning.<br /><br />3) I love Herzog's films. This film is essentially art appreciation, but knowing Herzog, I knew this would be a work of art itself. Most non-Herzog documentaries either want to inform you or persuade you: they deal in a medium of facts or arguments. Herzog, on the other hand, deals in a medium of metaphor, sound, and imagery. Rather than trying to tell you everything there is to know about the cave and its painters, he just wants to move you. He wants you to see/hear/feel why this matters. And he knows that words are instruments far too blunt for that task. He culls his words to the bare minimum; there are often 5+ minute stretches where there are no words at all.<br /><br />---<br /><br />I walked into the theater at the corner of a busy Manhattan block. When I walked out 90 minutes later, everything looked a little different. It was still the same busy Manhattan block, but now somehow it looked slower, more interesting, more incredible. I had the dizzying feeling of having just been psychospiritually rocked.<br /><br />Writing about this makes me near-desperate to see it again. I loved it. I put it right there with Encounters at the End of the World as my favorite Herzog doc. But Herzog docs are made to be seen in theaters. As badly as I want to see it again, I just can't do it. The early ad copy about this book, including some on Amazon, includes the term \\"Muggles,\\" yet the edition I have in hand avoids that word on the cover.<br />2. Another recipe explains how to boil oatmeal.<br /><br />I am just not certain about the value of the book. 1619492911|5.0|For those who have read F. Scott Fitzgerald's famous 1931 short story \\"Babylon Revisited,\\" this 1922 novel reads like a prequel, detailing the descent of a couple too beautiful for their own good. It's a mesmerizing, brilliantly written book that still leaves a mark.<br /><br />The Pagan Harleman introduction to my excellent Barnes & Noble edition notes \\"The Beautiful And Damned\\" is \\"Fitzgerald's least-known novel,\\" but it could also be his most intrinsic. Fitzgerald will, and should, always be known as the man who wrote \\"The Great Gatsby,\\" but this lines up more with what we think of when we think of him, of rich and bright young people throwing their lives away in search of elusive pleasures and verities.<br /><br />\\"Things are sweeter when they're lost,\\" observes the novel's main protagonist, Anthony Patch. \\"I know - because once I wanted something and got it. It was the only thing I ever wanted badly, Dot. And when I got it it turned to dust in my hands.\\"<br /><br />Patch is speaking here of the novel's other main character, his partner in dissolution, Gloria. If his first novel, \\"This Side Of Paradise,\\" serves as F. Scott Fitzgerald's literary introduction, \\"Beautiful And Damned\\" is when he unveiled the other half of his legacy, Zelda. As he writes of Gloria Gilbert, he might have written for Zelda Fitzgerald, \\"whose radiant fire and freshness was the living material of which the dead beauty of books was made.\\"<br /><br />Gloria doesn't exactly line up with the former Miss Sayre; at one point in the novel she brags about not reading books whereas Zelda was quite a literary-minded person. But as a Southern belle of decided caprice and high temper, Gloria clearly is based on Scott's then-new bride to a great degree, and that knowledge adds to the experience of reading this book. How did the bedroom conversations go while Zelda read this over his shoulder?<br /><br />Not too terribly, I think. Gloria is presented as a bit of a headcase and the butt of some comedy in the book's earlier, lightest third. As the novel develops, however, our sympathies move from Anthony to her. She's stronger, and despite her words, more reliable in action. Her worst fault may be in sticking with Anthony as long as she does.<br /><br />Anthony is the novel's problematic center, a shiftless layabout at the mercy of whatever wind catches him, content to await the inheritance of his grandfather's Midas-like legacy. Fitzgerald's great achievement here, other than his constant amazing flows of descriptive prose and his deft shifts from comedy to tragedy, is his ability to keep us sympathetic to Anthony despite his many failings. His plight is fully self-made, yet you feel for him like you might a moth at a candle.<br /><br />Are there flaws here? Yes, but they work in the context of the novel. Minor storylines regarding such things as the religious leanings of Gloria's mother disappear unresolved, while others like the interest in Gloria of a moviemaker mutate strangely into relative dead ends. These feel less like missteps while you are reading, though, and more like the rushing flow of life one experiences when young. Fitzgerald's racial and social biases are on stark display at times, but they reflect the thinking of his characters and the narrowness of their world, and so work, too.<br /><br />Most amazing to me was that Fitzgerald was only 25 and at the height of his fortune when he wrote this mature and prescient tale of trouble ahead. It's a fine companion to \\"Babylon Revisited\\" that way, too; a worried look forward rather than a wan look back.in the book's earlier, lightest third. As the novel develops, however, our sympathies move from Anthony to her. She's stronger, and despite her words, more reliable in action. Widower Mr Buxton (Jonathan Pryce), an old resident of Cranford, returns with his now-grown son William (Tom Hiddleston) and spirited ward Erminia (Michelle Dockery).  On the other side of the social divide is the lovely Peggy Bell (Jodie Whittaker).  Cranford's newest romance has a lot riding against it, though, with Buxton very vocal in his displeasure at Peggy eventually becoming William's wife.  Mrs Jamieson's sister-in-law Lady Glenmire (Celia Imrie) is another enchanting newcomer.<br /><br />After closing down her enterprising parlour tea shop, Miss Matty (Dame Judi Dench) has settled into the joyful role of being a second mother to Tilly, the little daughter of Martha and Jem (Claudie Blakley and Andrew Buchan), whilst her brother Peter (Nicholas Le Prevost) provides the necessary money to keep the Jenkyns household going. Miss Galindo (Emma Fielding) finally manages to secure Lady Ludlow's son Lord Septimus (Rory Kinnear) to return during a brief trip from his villa at Lake Lugano, but Lady Ludlow passes away before he arrives.  When Septimus unravels the bleak financial condition of the estate and realises that he'll have to eventually pay back Lady Ludlow's mortgage into the inheritance of young Harry Gregson (Alex Etel), he attempts to manipulate the boy into accepting a smaller immediate payment in lieu of the larger amount owed, underestimating the powerful ally that Harry has in Miss Galindo, and her fervent quest to see Mr Carter's last wishes carried out.<br /><br />The central dilemma in town remains the prospect of the railway \\"invading\\" the peace and calm of Cranford itself.  It's an argument which is pitting father against son, neighbour against neighbour, and driving away many valued workers, including Jem, who has become one of the local craftsmen drawn to the higher wages offered by the railroad company.  Despite her own fears and reservations, Miss Matty organises for her closest friends to experience rail travel first-hand, and unwittingly triggers a series of dramas with wide-reaching consequences for several Cranford residents...<br /><br />As in the original CRANFORD miniseries, there is much to love and savour in this all-too-brief sequel.  Screenwriter Heidi Thomas is one of my personal favourites and she is a great practitioner in the art of how to write for period drama. I know of no other costume drama writer who can write such brilliant comedy - and with the antics of Miss Pole, Mrs Forrester and Mrs Jamieson, Heidi Thomas is in her element.<br /><br />The performances remain utterly delightful.
